<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<p><b>Gertrude Stein</b> (February 3, 1874 â July 27, 1946) was an American novelist, poet, playwright, and art collector. Born in the <a href="/wiki/Allegheny_West_(Pittsburgh)" title="Allegheny West (Pittsburgh)">Allegheny West</a> neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, and raised in <a href="/wiki/Oakland,_California" title="Oakland, California">Oakland</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, Stein moved to <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> in 1903, and made France her home for the remainder of her life. She hosted a Paris salon, where the leading figures of modernism in literature and art, such as <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a>, <a href="/wiki/F._Scott_Fitzgerald" title="F. Scott Fitzgerald">F. Scott Fitzgerald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinclair_Lewis" title="Sinclair Lewis">Sinclair Lewis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">Henri Matisse</a> would meet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brbl-archive.library.yale.edu_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-brbl-archive.library.yale.edu-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1933, Stein published a kind-of memoir of her Paris years, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Autobiography_of_Alice_B._Toklas" title="The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas">The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas</a></i>, written in the voice of her <a href="/wiki/Life_partner" title="Life partner">life partner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alice_B._Toklas" title="Alice B. Toklas">Alice B. Toklas</a>, <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alice_Toklas_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alice_Toklas-2">[2]</a></sup> an American-born member of the <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">Parisian avant-garde</a>. The book became a literary bestseller and vaulted Stein from the relative obscurity of the cult-literature scene into the limelight of mainstream attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-3">[3]</a></sup> Two quotes from her works have become widely known: "<a href="/wiki/Rose_is_a_rose_is_a_rose_is_a_rose" title="Rose is a rose is a rose is a rose">Rose is a rose is a rose is a rose</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/There_is_no_there_there" title="There is no there there">there is no there there</a>", with the latter often taken to be a reference to her childhood home of <a href="/wiki/Oakland,_California" title="Oakland, California">Oakland, California</a>.</p>
<p>Her books include <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quod_Erat_Demonstrandum" title="Quod Erat Demonstrandum">Q.E.D. (Quod Erat Demonstrandum)</a></i> (1903), about a lesbian romantic affair involving several of Stein's female friends; <i><a href="/wiki/Fernhurst" title="Fernhurst">Fernhurst</a></i>, a fictional story about a romantic affair; <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Lives" title="Three Lives">Three Lives</a></i> (1905â06) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Making_of_Americans" title="The Making of Americans">The Making of Americans</a></i> (1902-1911). In <i><a href="/wiki/Tender_Buttons_(book)" title="Tender Buttons (book)">Tender Buttons</a></i> (1914), Stein commented on lesbian sexuality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackmer_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blackmer-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her activities during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> have been the subject of analysis and commentary. As a Jew living in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi" title="Nazi">Nazi</a>-occupied France, Stein may have only been able to sustain her lifestyle as an art collector, and indeed to ensure her physical safety, through the protection of the powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vichy_government" title="Vichy government">Vichy government</a> official and Nazi collaborator <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Fa%C3%BF" title="Bernard FaÃ¿">Bernard FaÃ¿</a>. After the war ended, Stein expressed admiration for another Nazi collaborator, Vichy leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marshall_P%C3%A9tain" title="Marshall PÃ©tain">Marshall PÃ©tain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[6]</a></sup> Some have argued that certain accounts of Stein's wartime activities have amounted to a "witch hunt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C4WDefault-7643746_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-C4WDefault-7643746-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p>Stein, the youngest of a family of five children, was born on February 3, 1874, in <a href="/wiki/Allegheny,_Pennsylvania" title="Allegheny, Pennsylvania">Allegheny, Pennsylvania</a> (which merged with <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> in 1907) to upper-middle-class <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish</a> parents, Daniel and Amelia Stein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[9]</a></sup> Her father was a wealthy businessman with real estate holdings. German and English were spoken in their home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:154px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gertrude_Stein_age_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="214" data-file-width="152" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Gertrude_Stein_age_3.jpg" width="152"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gertrude_Stein_age_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gertrude Stein, age 3</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Stein was three years old, she and her family moved to <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, and then Paris. Accompanied by governesses and tutors, the Steins endeavored to imbue their children with the cultured sensibilities of European history and life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thecrimson.com_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-thecrimson.com-11">[11]</a></sup> After a year-long sojourn abroad, they returned to America in 1878, settling in <a href="/wiki/Oakland,_California" title="Oakland, California">Oakland, California</a>, where her father became director of <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>'s street car lines, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Market_Street_Railway_(1893%E2%80%931944)" title="Market Street Railway (1893â1944)">Market Street Railway</a>, in an era when public transportation was a privately owned enterprise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Stein attended <a href="/wiki/Temple_Sinai_(Oakland,_California)" title="Temple Sinai (Oakland, California)">First Hebrew Congregation of Oakland</a>'s Sabbath school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenbaum21_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenbaum21-13">[13]</a></sup> During their residence in Oakland, they lived for four years on a ten-acre lot, and Stein built many memories of California there. She would often go on excursions with her brother, Leo, with whom she developed a close relationship. Stein found formal schooling in Oakland unstimulating, but she read often: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wordsworth" title="Wordsworth">Wordsworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Scott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Burns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Smollett" title="Tobias Smollett">Smollett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fielding" title="Henry Fielding">Fielding</a>, and more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Stein was 14 years old, her mother died. Three years later, her father died as well. Stein's eldest brother, Michael Stein, then took over the family business holdings and in 1892 arranged for Gertrude and another sister, Bertha, to live with their mother's family in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Here she lived with her uncle <a href="/wiki/David_Bachrach" title="David Bachrach">David Bachrach</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> who in 1877 had married Gertrude's maternal aunt, Fanny Keyser.</p>
<p>In Baltimore, Stein met <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claribel_Cone" title="Claribel Cone">Claribel</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etta_Cone" title="Etta Cone">Etta Cone</a>, who held Saturday evening <a href="/wiki/Salon_(gathering)" title="Salon (gathering)">salons</a> that she would later emulate in Paris.<br/>
The Cones shared an appreciation for art and conversation about it and modeled a domestic division of labor that Stein would replicate in her relationship with <a href="/wiki/Alice_B._Toklas" title="Alice B. Toklas">Alice B. Toklas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radcliffe">Radcliffe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Radcliffe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Stein attended <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_College" title="Radcliffe College">Radcliffe College</a>, then an annex of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, from 1893 to 1897 and was a student of psychologist <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a>. With James's supervision, Stein and another student, Leon Mendez Solomons, performed experiments on <i>normal motor automatism</i>, a phenomenon hypothesized to occur in people when their attention is divided between two simultaneous intelligent activities such as writing and speaking.</p>
<p>These experiments yielded examples of writing that appeared to represent "<a href="/wiki/Stream_of_consciousness_(psychology)" title="Stream of consciousness (psychology)">stream of consciousness</a>", a psychological theory often attributed to James and the style of modernist authors <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a>. In 1934, behavioral psychologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B.F._Skinner" title="B.F. Skinner">B.F. Skinner</a> interpreted Stein's difficult poem <i>Tender Buttons</i> as an example of <i>normal motor automatism</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In a letter Stein wrote during the 1930s, she explained that she never accepted the theory of <a href="/wiki/Automatic_writing" title="Automatic writing">automatic writing</a>: "[T]here can be automatic movements, but not automatic writing. Writing for the normal person is too complicated an activity to be indulged in automatically."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> She did publish an article in a psychological journal on "spontaneous automatic writing" while at Radcliffe, but "the unconscious and the intuition (even when James himself wrote about them) never concerned her."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>At Radcliffe, she began a lifelong friendship with Mabel Foote Weeks, whose correspondence traces much of the progression of Stein's life. In 1897, Stein spent the summer in <a href="/wiki/Woods_Hole,_Massachusetts" title="Woods Hole, Massachusetts">Woods Hole, Massachusetts</a>, studying embryology at the <a href="/wiki/Marine_Biological_Laboratory" title="Marine Biological Laboratory">Marine Biological Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hopkins_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-hopkins-19">[19]</a></sup> She received her A.B. (Bachelor of Arts) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magna_cum_laude" title="Magna cum laude">magna cum laude</a> from Radcliffe in 1898.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Johns_Hopkins_School_of_Medicine">Johns Hopkins School of Medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Johns Hopkins School of Medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>William James, who had become a committed mentor to Stein at Radcliffe, recognizing her intellectual potential, and declaring her his "most brilliant woman student", encouraged Stein to enroll in medical school. Although Stein professed no interest in either the theory or practice of medicine, she enrolled at <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_School_of_Medicine" title="Johns Hopkins School of Medicine">Johns Hopkins School of Medicine</a> in 1897. In her fourth year, Stein failed an important course, lost interest, and left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[9]</a></sup> Ultimately, medical school had bored her, and she had spent many of her evenings not applying herself to her studies, but taking long walks and attending the opera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thecrimson.com_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-thecrimson.com-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poetryfoundation.com_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-poetryfoundation.com-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stein's tenure at Johns Hopkins was marked by challenge and stress. Men dominated the medical field, and the inclusion of women in the profession was not unreservedly or unanimously welcomed. Writing of this period in her life (in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Things_As_They_Are" title="Things As They Are">Things As They Are</a></i>, 1903) Stein often revealed herself as a depressed young woman dealing with a paternalistic culture, struggling to find her own identity, which she realized could not conform to the conventional female role. Her uncorseted physical appearance and eccentric mode of dress aroused comment and she was described as "Big and floppy and sandaled and not caring a damn".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-22">[22]</a></sup> According to Linda Wagner-Martin, Stein's "controversial stance on women's medicine caused problems with the male faculty" and contributed to her decision to leave without finishing her degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Asked to give a lecture to a group of Baltimore women in 1899, Stein gave a controversial speech titled "The Value of College Education for Women", undoubtedly designed to provoke the largely middle-class audience. In the lecture Stein maintained:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"average middle class woman [supported by] some male relative, a husband or father or brother,...[is] not worth her keep economically considered. [This economic dependence caused her to become] oversexed...adapting herself to the abnormal sex desire of the male...and becoming a creature that should have been first a human being and then a woman into one that is a woman first and always."</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>ââ<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-22">[22]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>While a student at Johns Hopkins and purportedly still naÃ¯ve about sexual matters, Stein experienced an awakening of her latent sexuality. Sometime in 1899 or 1900, she became infatuated with <a href="/wiki/Mary_Bookstaver" title="Mary Bookstaver">Mary Bookstaver</a> who was involved in a relationship with a medical student, Mabel Haynes. Witnessing the relationship between the two women served for Stein as her "erotic awakening". The unhappy love triangle demoralized Stein, arguably contributing to her decision to abandon her medical studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-22">[22]</a></sup> In 1902 Stein's brother <a href="/wiki/Leo_Stein" title="Leo Stein">Leo Stein</a> left for <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, and Stein followed. The following year the two relocated to Paris, where Leo hoped to pursue an art career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poetryfoundation.com_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-poetryfoundation.com-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Matisse-Woman-with-a-Hat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1653" data-file-width="1214" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/Matisse-Woman-with-a-Hat.jpg/220px-Matisse-Woman-with-a-Hat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/Matisse-Woman-with-a-Hat.jpg/330px-Matisse-Woman-with-a-Hat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/Matisse-Woman-with-a-Hat.jpg/440px-Matisse-Woman-with-a-Hat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Matisse-Woman-with-a-Hat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">Henri Matisse</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Woman_with_a_Hat" title="Woman with a Hat">Woman with a Hat</a></i>, 1905. <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="San Francisco Museum of Modern Art">San Francisco Museum of Modern Art</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_collection">Art collection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Art collection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leo,_Gertrude,_Michael_Stein.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="197" data-file-width="256" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Leo%2C_Gertrude%2C_Michael_Stein.jpg/220px-Leo%2C_Gertrude%2C_Michael_Stein.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/67/Leo%2C_Gertrude%2C_Michael_Stein.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leo,_Gertrude,_Michael_Stein.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Leo, Gertrude and Michael Stein</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 1903 until 1914, when they dissolved their common household, Gertrude and her brother Leo shared living quarters near the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luxembourg_Garden" title="Luxembourg Garden">Luxembourg Garden</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_Bank" title="Left Bank">Left Bank</a> of Paris in a two-story apartment (with adjacent studio) located on the interior courtyard at <a href="/wiki/27_rue_de_Fleurus" title="27 rue de Fleurus">27 rue de Fleurus</a>, <a href="/wiki/6th_arrondissement_of_Paris" title="6th arrondissement of Paris">6th arrondissement</a>. Here they accumulated the works of art that formed a collection that became renowned for its prescience and historical importance.</p>
<p>The gallery space was furnished with imposing, <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> era furniture manufactured in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. The paintings lined the walls in tiers trailing many feet to the ceiling. Initially illuminated by gaslight, the artwork was later lit by electric light shortly prior to <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Leo Stein cultivated important art world connections, enabling the Stein holdings to grow over time. The art historian and collector <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Berenson" title="Bernard Berenson">Bernard Berenson</a> hosted Gertrude and Leo in his English country house in 1902, facilitating their introduction to <a href="/wiki/Paul_C%C3%A9zanne" title="Paul CÃ©zanne">Paul CÃ©zanne</a> and the dealer <a href="/wiki/Ambroise_Vollard" title="Ambroise Vollard">Ambroise Vollard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Vollard was heavily involved in the CÃ©zanne art market, and he was the first important contact in the Paris art world for both Leo and Gertrude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The joint collection of Gertrude and Leo Stein began in late 1904 when Michael Stein announced that their trust account had accumulated a balance of 8,000 francs. They spent this at <a href="/wiki/Ambroise_Vollard" title="Ambroise Vollard">Vollard</a>'s Gallery, buying <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gauguin" title="Paul Gauguin">Gauguin</a>'s <i>Sunflowers</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and <i>Three Tahitians</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> CÃ©zanne's <i>Bathers</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and two <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir" title="Pierre-Auguste Renoir">Renoirs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gertrude_Stein_sitting_on_a_sofa_in_her_Paris_studio_-_Library_of_Congress.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6102" data-file-width="4942" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Gertrude_Stein_sitting_on_a_sofa_in_her_Paris_studio_-_Library_of_Congress.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Gertrude_Stein_sitting_on_a_sofa_in_her_Paris_studio_-_Library_of_Congress.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Gertrude_Stein_sitting_on_a_sofa_in_her_Paris_studio_-_Library_of_Congress.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Gertrude_Stein_sitting_on_a_sofa_in_her_Paris_studio_-_Library_of_Congress.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Gertrude_Stein_sitting_on_a_sofa_in_her_Paris_studio_-_Library_of_Congress.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Gertrude_Stein_sitting_on_a_sofa_in_her_Paris_studio_-_Library_of_Congress.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gertrude_Stein_sitting_on_a_sofa_in_her_Paris_studio_-_Library_of_Congress.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gertrude Stein sitting on a sofa in her Paris studio, with a portrait of her by Pablo Picasso, and other modern art paintings hanging on the wall (before 1910)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The art collection increased and the walls at Rue de Fleurus were rearranged continually to make way for new acquisitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In "the first half of 1905" the Steins acquired <a href="/wiki/Paul_C%C3%A9zanne" title="Paul CÃ©zanne">CÃ©zanne</a>'s <i>Portrait of Mme CÃ©zanne</i> and <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="EugÃ¨ne Delacroix">Delacroix</a>'s <i>Perseus and Andromeda</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Shortly after the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Salon_d%27Automne" title="Salon d'Automne">Salon d'Automne</a> of 1905 (on October 18, 1905), the Steins acquired Matisse's <i>Woman with a Hat</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and Picasso's <i>Young Girl with Basket of Flowers</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Henry_McBride_(art_critic)" title="Henry McBride (art critic)">Henry McBride</a> (art critic for the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sun_(New_York)" title="The Sun (New York)">New York Sun</a></i>) did much for Stein's reputation in the United States, publicizing her art acquisitions and her importance as a cultural figure. Of the art collection at 27 Rue de Fleurus, McBride commented: "[I]n proportion to its size and quality... [it is] just about the most potent of any that I have ever heard of in history."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mellow_193_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mellow_193-33">[33]</a></sup> McBride also made the observation that Gertrude "collected geniuses rather than masterpieces. She recognized them a long way off."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mellow_193_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mellow_193-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>By early 1906, Leo and Gertrude Stein's studio had many paintings by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Manguin" title="Henri Manguin">Henri Manguin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Bonnard" title="Pierre Bonnard">Pierre Bonnard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_C%C3%A9zanne" title="Paul CÃ©zanne">Paul CÃ©zanne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir" title="Pierre-Auguste Renoir">Pierre-Auguste Renoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier" title="HonorÃ© Daumier">HonorÃ© Daumier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">Henri Matisse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Henri_de_Toulouse-Lautrec" title="Henri de Toulouse-Lautrec">Henri de Toulouse-Lautrec</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Their collection was representative of two famous art exhibitions that took place during their residence together in Paris, and to which they contributed, either by lending their art, or by patronizing the featured artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The Steins' elder brother, Michael, and sister-in-law Sarah (Sally) acquired a large number of <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">Henri Matisse</a> paintings; Gertrude's friends from Baltimore, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claribel_Cone" title="Claribel Cone">Claribel</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etta_Cone" title="Etta Cone">Etta Cone</a>, collected similarly, eventually donating their art collection, virtually intact, to the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Museum_of_Art" title="Baltimore Museum of Art">Baltimore Museum of Art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>While numerous artists visited the Stein salon, many of these artists were not represented among the paintings on the walls at 27 Rue de Fleurus. Where <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir" title="Pierre-Auguste Renoir">Renoir</a>, CÃ©zanne, Matisse, and Picasso's works dominated Leo and Gertrude's collection, the collection of Michael and <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Stein" title="Sarah Stein">Sarah Stein</a> emphasized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matisse" title="Matisse">Matisse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In April 1914 Leo relocated to <a href="/wiki/Settignano" title="Settignano">Settignano, Italy, near Florence</a>, and the art collection was divided. The division of the Steins' art collection was described in a letter by Leo:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The CÃ©zanne apples have a unique importance to me that nothing can replace. The Picasso landscape is not important in any such sense. We are, as it seems to me on the whole, both so well off now that we needn't repine. The CÃ©zanne's had to be divided. I am willing to leave you the Picasso oeuvre, as you left me the Renoir, and you can have everything except that. I want to keep the few drawings that I have. This leaves no string for me, it is financially equable either way for estimates are only rough &amp; ready methods, &amp; I'm afraid you'll have to look upon the loss of the apples as an act of God. I have been anxious above all things that each should have in reason all that he wanted, and just as I was glad that Renoir was sufficiently indifferent to you so that you were ready to give them up, so I am glad that Pablo is sufficiently indifferent to me that I am willing to let you have all you want of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Leo departed with sixteen Renoirs, and relinquishing the Picassos and most of Matisse to his sister, took only a portrait sketch Picasso had done of him. He remained dedicated to CÃ©zanne, nonetheless, leaving all the artist's works with his sister, taking with him only a CÃ©zanne painting of "5 apples".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-23">[23]</a></sup> The split between brother and sister was acrimonious. Stein did not see Leo Stein again until after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, and then through only a brief greeting on the street in Paris. After this accidental encounter, they never saw or spoke to each other again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-23">[23]</a></sup> The Steins' holdings were dispersed eventually by various methods and for various reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> After Stein's and Leo's households separated in 1914, she continued to collect examples of Picasso's art, which had turned to <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">Cubism</a>, a style Leo did not appreciate. At her death, Gertrude's remaining collection emphasized the artwork of Picasso and <a href="/wiki/Juan_Gris" title="Juan Gris">Juan Gris</a>, most of her other pictures having been sold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoMA_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-MoMA-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gertrude Stein's personality has dominated the provenance of the Stein art legacy. It was, however, her brother Leo who was the astute art appraiser. Alfred Barr Jr., the founding director of New York's <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">Museum of Modern Art</a>, said that between the years of 1905 and 1907, "[Leo] was possibly the most discerning connoisseur and collector of 20th century painting in the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> After the artworks were divided between the two Stein siblings, it was Gertrude who moved on to champion the works of what proved to be lesser talents in the 1930s. She concentrated on the work of <a href="/wiki/Juan_Gris" title="Juan Gris">Juan Gris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Masson" title="AndrÃ© Masson">AndrÃ© Masson</a>, and Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Cyril_Rose" title="Francis Cyril Rose">Francis Rose</a>. In 1932, Stein asserted: "painting now after its great period has come back to be a minor art."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1945, in a preface for the first exhibition of Spanish painter <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Riba_Rovira" title="Francisco Riba Rovira">Francisco Riba Rovira</a> (who painted a portrait of her), Stein wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>I explained that for me, all modern painting is based on what CÃ©zanne nearly made, instead of basing itself on what he almost managed to make. When he could not make a thing, he hijacked it and left it. He insisted on showing his incapacity: he spread his lack of success: showing what he could not do, became an obsession for him. People influenced by him were also obsessed by the things which they could not reach and they began the system of camouflage. It was natural to do so, even inevitable: that soon became an art, in peace and in war, and Matisse concealed and insisted at the same time on that CÃ©zanne could not realize, and Picasso concealed, played and tormented all these things. The only one who wanted to insist on this problem, was Juan Gris. He persisted by deepening the things which CÃ©zanne wanted to do, but it was too hard a task for him: it killed him. And now here we are, I find a young painter who does not follow the tendency to play with what CÃ©zanne could not do, but who attacks any right the things which he tried to make, to create the objects which have to exist, for, and in themselves, and not in relation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="27_rue_de_Fleurus:_The_Stein_salon">27 rue de Fleurus: The Stein salon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 27 rue de Fleurus: The Stein salon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plaque_Gertrude_Stein,_27_rue_de_Fleurus,_Paris_6.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2070" data-file-width="2275" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Plaque_Gertrude_Stein%2C_27_rue_de_Fleurus%2C_Paris_6.jpg/220px-Plaque_Gertrude_Stein%2C_27_rue_de_Fleurus%2C_Paris_6.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Plaque_Gertrude_Stein%2C_27_rue_de_Fleurus%2C_Paris_6.jpg/330px-Plaque_Gertrude_Stein%2C_27_rue_de_Fleurus%2C_Paris_6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Plaque_Gertrude_Stein%2C_27_rue_de_Fleurus%2C_Paris_6.jpg/440px-Plaque_Gertrude_Stein%2C_27_rue_de_Fleurus%2C_Paris_6.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plaque_Gertrude_Stein,_27_rue_de_Fleurus,_Paris_6.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Plaque at 27 rue de Fleurus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The gatherings in the Stein home "brought together confluences of talent and thinking that would help define modernism in literature and art". Dedicated attendees included <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a>, <a href="/wiki/F._Scott_Fitzgerald" title="F. Scott Fitzgerald">F. Scott Fitzgerald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinclair_Lewis" title="Sinclair Lewis">Sinclair Lewis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Williamson_(harpsichordist)" title="Gavin Williamson (harpsichordist)">Gavin Williamson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thornton_Wilder" title="Thornton Wilder">Thornton Wilder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sherwood_Anderson" title="Sherwood Anderson">Sherwood Anderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Cyril_Rose" title="Francis Cyril Rose">Francis Cyril Rose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brown_(writer,_poet,_publisher)" title="Bob Brown (writer, poet, publisher)">Bob Brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Crevel" title="RenÃ© Crevel">RenÃ© Crevel</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lisabeth_de_Gramont" title="Ãlisabeth de Gramont">Ãlisabeth de Gramont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Picabia" title="Francis Picabia">Francis Picabia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claribel_Cone" title="Claribel Cone">Claribel Cone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mildred_Aldrich" title="Mildred Aldrich">Mildred Aldrich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Van_Vechten" title="Carl Van Vechten">Carl Van Vechten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">Henri Matisse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brbl-archive.library.yale.edu_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-brbl-archive.library.yale.edu-1">[1]</a></sup> Saturday evenings had been set as the fixed day and time for formal congregation so Stein could work at her writing uninterrupted by impromptu visitors. It was Stein's partner Alice who became the de facto hostess for the wives and girlfriends of the artists in attendance, who met in a separate room.</p>
<p>Gertrude attributed the beginnings of the Saturday evening salons to Matisse, as people began visiting to see his paintings and those of CÃ©zanne: "Matisse brought people, everybody brought somebody, and they came at any time and it began to be a nuisance, and it was in this way that Saturday evenings began."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among Picasso's acquaintances who frequented the Saturday evenings were: <a href="/wiki/Fernande_Olivier" title="Fernande Olivier">Fernande Olivier</a> (Picasso's mistress), <a href="/wiki/Georges_Braque" title="Georges Braque">Georges Braque</a> (artist), <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Derain" title="AndrÃ© Derain">AndrÃ© Derain</a> (artist), <a href="/wiki/Max_Jacob" title="Max Jacob">Max Jacob</a> (poet), <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Apollinaire" title="Guillaume Apollinaire">Guillaume Apollinaire</a> (poet), <a href="/wiki/Marie_Laurencin" title="Marie Laurencin">Marie Laurencin</a> (artist, and Apollinaire's mistress), <a href="/wiki/Henri_Rousseau" title="Henri Rousseau">Henri Rousseau</a> (painter), and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stella" title="Joseph Stella">Joseph Stella</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hemingway frequented Stein's salon, but the two had an uneven relationship. They began as close friends, with Hemingway admiring Stein as a mentor, but they later grew apart, especially after Stein called Hemingway "yellow" in <i>The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[9]</a></sup> Upon the birth of his son, Hemingway asked Stein to be the godmother of his child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyers1_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meyers1-47">[47]</a></sup> While Stein has been credited with inventing the term "<a href="/wiki/Lost_Generation" title="Lost Generation">Lost Generation</a>" for those whose defining moment in time and coming of age had been World War I and its aftermath, there are at least three versions of the story that led to the phrase, two by Hemingway and one by Stein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the summer of 1931, Stein advised the young composer and writer <a href="/wiki/Paul_Bowles" title="Paul Bowles">Paul Bowles</a> to go to <a href="/wiki/Tangier" title="Tangier">Tangier</a>, where she and Alice had vacationed.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_style">Literary style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Literary style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gertrude_stein.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3736" data-file-width="2928" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Gertrude_stein.jpg/220px-Gertrude_stein.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Gertrude_stein.jpg/330px-Gertrude_stein.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Gertrude_stein.jpg/440px-Gertrude_stein.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gertrude_stein.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Carl_Van_Vechten" title="Carl Van Vechten">Carl Van Vechten</a>, <i>Portrait of Gertrude Stein</i>, 1934</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Stein's writing can be placed in three categories: "hermetic" works best illustrated by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Making_of_Americans" title="The Making of Americans">The Making of Americans</a>: The Hersland Family</i>; popularized writing such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Autobiography_of_Alice_B._Toklas" title="The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas">The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas</a></i>; and speech writing and more accessible autobiographical writing of later years, of which <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Brewsie_and_Willie&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Brewsie and Willie (page does not exist)">Brewsie and Willie</a></i> is a good example. Her works include novels, plays, stories, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Libretti" title="Libretti">libretti</a> and poems written in a highly idiosyncratic, playful, repetitive, and humorous style. Typical quotes are: "<a href="/wiki/Rose_is_a_rose_is_a_rose_is_a_rose" title="Rose is a rose is a rose is a rose">Rose is a rose is a rose is a rose</a>"; "Out of kindness comes redness and out of rudeness comes rapid same question, out of an eye comes research, out of selection comes painful cattle"; about her childhood home in <a href="/wiki/Oakland,_California" title="Oakland, California">Oakland</a>, "There is no there there"; and "The change of color is likely and a difference a very little difference is prepared. Sugar is not a vegetable."</p>
<p>These <a href="/wiki/Stream_of_consciousness_(narrative_mode)" title="Stream of consciousness (narrative mode)">stream-of-consciousness</a> experiments, rhythmical essays or "portraits", were designed to evoke "the excitingness of pure being" and can be seen as literature's answer to visual art styles and forms such as <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">Cubism</a>, plasticity, and <a href="/wiki/Collage" title="Collage">collage</a>. Many of the experimental works such as <i>Tender Buttons</i> have since been interpreted by critics as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feminist" title="Feminist">feminist</a> reworking of patriarchal language. These works were well received by avant-garde critics but did not initially achieve mainstream success. Despite Stein's work on "<a href="/wiki/Automatic_writing" title="Automatic writing">automatic writing</a>" with <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a>, she did not see her work as automatic, but as an 'excess of consciousness'.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Though Gertrude collected cubist paintings, especially those of Picasso, the largest visual arts influence on her literary work is that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C3%A9zanne" title="CÃ©zanne">CÃ©zanne</a>. Particularly, he influenced her idea of equality, distinguished from universality: "the whole field of the canvas is important" (p.Â 8<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A more detailed citation is required. (November 2012)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>). Rather than a figure/ground relationship, "Stein in her work with words used the entire text as a field in which every element mattered as much as any other." It is a subjective relationship that includes multiple viewpoints. Stein explained: "The important thing is that you must have deep down as the deepest thing in you a sense of equality."</p>
<p>Her use of repetition is ascribed to her search for descriptions of the "bottom nature" of her characters, such as in <i>The Making of Americans</i> where the narrator is described through the repetition of narrative phrases such as "As I was saying" and "There will be now a history of her." Stein used many Anglo-Saxon words and avoided words with "too much association". Social judgement is absent in her writing, so the reader is given the power to decide how to think and feel about the writing. Anxiety, fear and anger are also absent, and her work is harmonic and integrative.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Stein predominantly used the present progressive tense, creating a continuous present in her work, which Grahn argues is a consequence of the previous principles, especially commonality and centeredness. Grahn describes "play" as the granting of autonomy and agency to the readers or audience: "rather than the <a href="/wiki/Psychological_manipulation" title="Psychological manipulation">emotional manipulation</a> that is a characteristic of linear writing, Stein uses <i>play</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> In addition Stein's work is funny, and multilayered, allowing a variety of interpretations and engagements. Lastly Grahn argues that one must "<i>inster</i>stand... engage with the work, to mix with it in an active engagement, rather than 'figuring it out.' Figure it in."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In 1932, using an accessible style to appeal to a wider audience, she wrote <i>The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas</i>; the book would become her first best-seller. Despite the title, it was actually Stein's autobiography. The style was quite similar to that of <i>The Alice B. Toklas Cookbook</i>, which was written by Toklas.</p>
<p>Several of Stein's writings have been set to music by composers, including <a href="/wiki/Virgil_Thomson" title="Virgil Thomson">Virgil Thomson</a>'s operas <i><a href="/wiki/Four_Saints_in_Three_Acts" title="Four Saints in Three Acts">Four Saints in Three Acts</a></i> and <i>The Mother of Us All</i>, and <a href="/wiki/James_Tenney" title="James Tenney">James Tenney</a>'s setting of <i>Rose is a rose is a rose is a rose</i> as a <a href="/wiki/Canon_(music)" title="Canon (music)">canon</a> dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Philip_Corner" title="Philip Corner">Philip Corner</a>, beginning with "a" on an upbeat and continuing so that each repetition shuffles the words, e.g. "a/rose is a rose/is a rose is/a rose is a/rose."</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_career">Literary career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Literary career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GertrudeStein.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="999" data-file-width="816" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/GertrudeStein.JPG/220px-GertrudeStein.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/GertrudeStein.JPG/330px-GertrudeStein.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/GertrudeStein.JPG/440px-GertrudeStein.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GertrudeStein.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a>, <i>Portrait of Gertrude Stein</i>, 1906, <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a>, New York. When someone commented that Stein didn't look like her portrait, Picasso replied, "She will".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Stein wrote "<a href="/wiki/If_I_Told_Him:_A_Completed_Portrait_of_Picasso" title="If I Told Him: A Completed Portrait of Picasso">If I Told Him: A Completed Portrait of Picasso</a>" in response to the painting.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F%C3%A9lix_Valloton,_Portrait_of_Gertrude_Stein,_1907.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="431" data-file-width="352" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/F%C3%A9lix_Valloton%2C_Portrait_of_Gertrude_Stein%2C_1907.jpg/220px-F%C3%A9lix_Valloton%2C_Portrait_of_Gertrude_Stein%2C_1907.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/F%C3%A9lix_Valloton%2C_Portrait_of_Gertrude_Stein%2C_1907.jpg/330px-F%C3%A9lix_Valloton%2C_Portrait_of_Gertrude_Stein%2C_1907.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ab/F%C3%A9lix_Valloton%2C_Portrait_of_Gertrude_Stein%2C_1907.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F%C3%A9lix_Valloton,_Portrait_of_Gertrude_Stein,_1907.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_Vallotton" title="FÃ©lix Vallotton">FÃ©lix Vallotton</a>, <i>Portrait of Gertrude Stein</i>, 1907</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While living in Paris, Stein began submitting her writing for publication. Her earliest writings were mainly retellings of her college experiences. Her first critically acclaimed publication was <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Lives" title="Three Lives">Three Lives</a></i>. In 1911, <a href="/wiki/Mildred_Aldrich" title="Mildred Aldrich">Mildred Aldrich</a> introduced Stein to <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Dodge_Luhan" title="Mabel Dodge Luhan">Mabel Dodge Luhan</a> and they began a short-lived but fruitful friendship during which the wealthy Mabel Dodge promoted Gertrude's legend in the United States.</p>
<p>Mabel was enthusiastic about Stein's sprawling publication <i>The Makings of Americans</i> and, at a time when Stein had much difficulty selling her writing to publishers, privately published 300 copies of <i>Portrait of Mabel Dodge at Villa Curonia</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoMA_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-MoMA-41">[41]</a></sup> a copy of which was valued at $25,000 in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Dodge was also involved in the publicity and planning of the 69th <a href="/wiki/Armory_Show" title="Armory Show">Armory Show</a> in 1913, "the first avant-garde art exhibition in America".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoMA_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-MoMA-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, she wrote the first critical analysis of Stein's writing to appear in America, in "Speculations, or Post-Impressionists in Prose", published in a special March 1913 publication of <i>Arts and Decoration</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mellow_170_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mellow_170-53">[53]</a></sup> Foreshadowing Stein's later critical reception, Dodge wrote in "Speculations":</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In Gertrude Stein's writing every word lives and, apart from concept, it is so exquisitely rhythmical and cadenced that if we read it aloud and receive it as pure sound, it is like a kind of sensuous music. Just as one may stop, for once, in a way, before a canvas of Picasso, and, letting one's reason sleep for an instant, may exclaim: "It <i>is</i> a fine pattern!" so, listening to Gertrude Stein's words and forgetting to try to understand what they mean, one submits to their gradual charm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mellow_170_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mellow_170-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Stein and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Van_Vechten" title="Carl Van Vechten">Carl Van Vechten</a>, the noted critic and photographer, became acquainted in Paris in 1913. The two became lifelong friends, devising pet names for each other: Van Vechten was "Papa Woojums", and Stein, "Baby Woojums". Van Vechten served as an enthusiastic champion of Stein's literary work in the United States, in effect becoming her American agent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brbl-archive.library.yale.edu_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-brbl-archive.library.yale.edu-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="America_.281934.E2.80.931935.29">America (1934â1935)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: America (1934â1935)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In October 1934, Stein arrived in America after a 30-year absence. Disembarking from the ocean liner in New York, she encountered a throng of reporters. Front-page articles on Stein appeared in almost every New York City newspaper. As she rode through Manhattan to her hotel, she was able get a sense of the publicity that would hallmark her US tour. An electric sign in <a href="/wiki/Times_Square" title="Times Square">Times Square</a> announced to all that "Gertrude Stein Has Arrived".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-54">[54]</a></sup> Her six-month tour of the country encompassed 191 days of travel, criss-crossing 23 states and visiting 37 cities. Stein prepared her lectures for each stop-over in a formally structured way, and the audience was limited to five hundred attendees for each venue. She spoke, reading from notes, and provided for an audience question and answer period at the end of her presentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stein's effectiveness as a lecture speaker received varying evaluations. At the time, some maintained that "Stein's audiences by and large did not understand her lectures." Some of those in the psychiatric community weighed in, judging that Stein suffered from a <a href="/wiki/Speech_disorder" title="Speech disorder">speech disorder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palilalia" title="Palilalia">palilalia</a>, which caused her "to stutter over words and phrases". The predominant feeling, however, was that Stein was a compelling presence, a fascinating personality who had the ability to hold listeners with the "musicality of her language".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Washington, D.C. Stein was invited to have tea with the President's wife, <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beverly_Hills" title="Beverly Hills">Beverly Hills</a>, California, she visited actor and filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a> who reportedly discussed the future of cinema with her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-54">[54]</a></sup> Stein left America in May 1935, a newly minted American celebrity with a commitment from <a href="/wiki/Random_House" title="Random House">Random House</a>, who had agreed to become the American publisher for all of her future works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicago_Daily_Tribune" title="Chicago Daily Tribune">Chicago Daily Tribune</a></i> wrote after Stein's return to Paris: "No writer in years has been so widely discussed, so much caricatured, so passionately championed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Books">Books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Q.E.D."><i>Q.E.D</i>.</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Q.E.D.">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Gertrude completed <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quod_Erat_Demonstrandum" title="Quod Erat Demonstrandum">Q.E.D. (Quod Erat Demonstrandum)</a></i> on October 24, 1903.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> One of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Coming_out" title="Coming out">coming out</a> stories<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, it is about a romantic affair involving Stein and her friends Mabel Haynes, Grace Lounsbury and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Bookstaver" title="Mary Bookstaver">Mary Bookstaver</a>, and occurred between 1897 and 1901 while she was studying at <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins</a> in Baltimore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackmer_1995_pp._681_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blackmer_1995_pp._681-57">[57]</a></sup> This story is discussed more completely later in this article in the section on her <a href="/wiki/Gertrude_Stein#Lesbian_relationships" title="Gertrude Stein">lesbian relationships</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fernhurst_.281904.29"><i>Fernhurst</i> (1904)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fernhurst (1904)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1904 Stein began <i><a href="/wiki/Fernhurst" title="Fernhurst">Fernhurst</a></i>, a fictional account of a scandalous three-person romantic affair involving a dean (<a href="/wiki/M._Carey_Thomas" title="M. Carey Thomas">M. Carey Thomas</a>), a faculty member from <a href="/wiki/Bryn_Mawr_College" title="Bryn Mawr College">Bryn Mawr College</a> (Mary Gwinn) and a Harvard graduate (Alfred Hodder).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Mellow asserts that <i>Fernhurst</i> "is a decidedly minor and awkward piece of writing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mellow_67_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mellow_67-59">[59]</a></sup> It includes some commentary that Gertrude mentioned in her autobiography when she discussed the "fateful twenty-ninth year"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mellow_67_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mellow_67-59">[59]</a></sup> during which:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>All the forces that have been engaged through the years of childhood, adolescence and youth in confused and ferocious combat range themselves in ordered ranks (and during which) the straight and narrow gateway of maturity, and life which was all uproar and confusion narrows down to form and purpose, and we exchange a great dim possibility for a small hard reality.</p>
<p>Also in our American life where there is no coercion in custom and it is our right to change our vocation so often as we have desire and opportunity, it is a common experience that our youth extends through the whole first twenty-nine years of our life and it is not till we reach thirty that we find at last that vocation for which we feel ourselves fit and to which we willingly devote continued labor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Mellow observes that, in 1904, 30-year-old Gertrude "had evidently determined that the 'small hard reality' of her life would be writing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_Lives_.281905.E2.80.931906.29"><i>Three Lives</i> (1905â1906)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Three Lives (1905â1906)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Stein attributed the inception of <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Lives" title="Three Lives">Three Lives</a></i> to the inspiration she received from a portrait <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C3%A9zanne" title="CÃ©zanne">CÃ©zanne</a> had painted of his wife and which was in the Stein collection. She credited this as a <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelatory moment</a> in the evolution of her writing style. Stein described:</p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">â</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">that the stylistic method of <i>(Three Lives)</i> had been influenced by the CÃ©zanne portrait under which she sat writing. The portrait of Madame CÃ©zanne is one of the monumental examples of the artist's method, each exacting, carefully negotiated planeâfrom the suave reds of the armchair and the gray blues of the sitter's jacket to the vaguely figured wallpaper of the backgroundâhaving been structured into existence, seeming to fix the subject for all eternity. So it was with Gertrude's repetitive sentences, each one building up, phrase by phrase, the substance of her characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">â</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>She began <i>Three Lives</i> during the spring of 1905 and finished it the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Making_of_Americans_.281902.E2.80.931911.29"><i>The Making of Americans</i> (1902â1911)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: The Making of Americans (1902â1911)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Gertrude Stein stated the date for her writing of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Making_of_Americans" title="The Making of Americans">The Making of Americans</a></i> was 1906â8. Her biographer has uncovered evidence that it actually began in 1902 and did not end until 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Stein compared her work to <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ulysses_(novel)" title="Ulysses (novel)">Ulysses</a></i> and to <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Proust" title="Marcel Proust">Marcel Proust</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/In_Search_of_Lost_Time" title="In Search of Lost Time">In Search of Lost Time</a></i>. Her critics were less enthusiastic about it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mellow_1974.2C_p._122_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mellow_1974.2C_p._122-65">[65]</a></sup> Stein wrote the bulk of the novel between 1903 and 1911, and evidence from her manuscripts suggests three major periods of revision during that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The manuscript remained mostly hidden from public view until 1924 when, at the urging of <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ford_Madox_Ford" title="Ford Madox Ford">Ford Madox Ford</a> agreed to publish excerpts in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transatlantic_review" title="Transatlantic review">transatlantic review</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In 1925, the Paris-based Contact Press published a limited run of the novel consisting of 500 copies. A much-abridged edition was published by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harcourt_Brace" title="Harcourt Brace">Harcourt Brace</a> in 1934, but the full version remained out of print until <a href="/wiki/Something_Else_Press" title="Something Else Press">Something Else Press</a> republished it in 1966. In 1995, a new, definitive edition was published by <a href="/wiki/Dalkey_Archive_Press" title="Dalkey Archive Press">Dalkey Archive Press</a> with a foreword by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Gass" title="William Gass">William Gass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gertrude's <i>Matisse</i> and <i>Picasso</i> descriptive essays appeared in <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Stieglitz" title="Alfred Stieglitz">Alfred Stieglitz</a>'s August, 1912 edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Camera_Work" title="Camera Work">Camera Work</a></i>, a special edition devoted to Picasso and Matisse, and represented her very first publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kellner_266_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kellner_266-69">[69]</a></sup> Of this publication, Gertrude said, "[h]e was the first one that ever printed anything that I had done. And you can imagine what that meant to me or to any one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kellner_266_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kellner_266-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Word_Portraits_.281908.E2.80.931913.29">Word Portraits (1908â1913)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Word Portraits (1908â1913)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Stein's descriptive essays apparently began with her essay of Alice B. Toklas, "a little prose vignette, a kind of happy inspiration that had detached itself from the torrential prose of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Making_of_Americans" title="The Making of Americans">The Making of Americans</a></i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Stein's early efforts at word portraits are catalogued in <a href="#CITEREFMellow1974">Mellow (1974</a>, pp.Â 129â37) and under individual's names in Kellner, 1988. Matisse and Picasso were subjects of early essays,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> later collected and published in <i>Geography and Plays</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStein1922_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStein1922-72">[72]</a></sup> and <i>Portraits and Prayers</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStein1934_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStein1934-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her subjects included several ultimately famous personages, and her subjects provided a description of what she observed in her Saturday salons at 27 Rue de Fleurus: "Ada" (<a href="/wiki/Alice_B._Toklas" title="Alice B. Toklas">Alice B. Toklas</a>), "Two Women" (The <a href="/wiki/Cone_sisters" title="Cone sisters">Cone sisters</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claribel_Cone" title="Claribel Cone">Claribel Cone</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etta_Cone" title="Etta Cone">Etta Cone</a>), <i>Miss Furr and Miss Skeene</i> (Ethel Mars and Maud Hunt Squire), "Men" (<a href="/wiki/Hutchins_Hapgood" title="Hutchins Hapgood">Hutchins Hapgood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_David_Edstrom" title="Peter David Edstrom">Peter David Edstrom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Sterne" title="Maurice Sterne">Maurice Sterne</a>), "Matisse" (1909, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">Henri Matisse</a>), "Picasso" (1909, <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a>), "Portrait of Mabel Dodge at the Villa Curonia" (1911, <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Dodge_Luhan" title="Mabel Dodge Luhan">Mabel Dodge Luhan</a>), and "<a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Apollinaire" title="Guillaume Apollinaire">Guillaume Apollinaire</a>" (1913).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tender_Buttons_.281912.29"><i>Tender Buttons</i> (1912)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Tender Buttons (1912)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Tender_Buttons_(book)" title="Tender Buttons (book)">Tender Buttons</a></i> is the best known of Stein's "hermetic" works. It is a small book separated into three sectionsâ"Food, Objects and Rooms", each containing prose under subtitles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Its publication in 1914 caused a great dispute between <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Dodge_Luhan" title="Mabel Dodge Luhan">Mabel Dodge Luhan</a> and Stein, because Mabel had been working to have it published by another publisher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mellow_178_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mellow_178-77">[77]</a></sup> Mabel wrote at length about what she viewed as the bad choice of publishing it with the press Gertrude selected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mellow_178_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mellow_178-77">[77]</a></sup> Evans wrote Gertrude:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Claire Marie Press... is absolutely third rate, &amp; in bad odor here, being called for the most part 'decadent" and Broadwayish and that sort of thing... I think it would be a pity to publish with [Claire Marie Press] <i>if</i> it will emphasize the idea in the opinion of the public, that there is something degenerate &amp; effete &amp; decadent about the whole of the cubist movement which they <i>all</i> connect you with, because, hang it all, as long as they don't understand a thing they think all sorts of things. My feeling in this is quite strong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mellow_178_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mellow_178-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Stein ignored Mabel's exhortations, and eventually Mabel, and published 1,000 copies of the book, in 1914. An antiquarian copy was valued at over $1,200 in 2007. It is currently in print, and was re-released as <i>Tender Buttons: The Corrected Centennial Edition</i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_Lights_Publishers" title="City Lights Publishers">City Lights Publishers</a> in March 2014.</p>
<p>In an interview with <a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Bartlett_Haas" title="de:Robert Bartlett Haas">Robert Bartlett Haas</a> in "A Transatlantic Interview - 1946", Stein insisted that this work was completely "realistic" in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Flaubert" title="Gustave Flaubert">Gustave Flaubert</a>, stating the following: "I used to take objects on a table, like a tumbler or any kind of object and try to get the picture of it clear and separate in my mind and create a word relationship between the word and the things seen." Commentators have indicated that what she meant was that the <i>reference</i> of objects remained central to her work, although the <i>representation</i> of them had not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perloff_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perloff-78">[78]</a></sup> Scholar <a href="/wiki/Marjorie_Perloff" title="Marjorie Perloff">Marjorie Perloff</a> had said of Stein that "[u]nlike her contemporaries (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T.S._Eliot" title="T.S. Eliot">Eliot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marianne_Moore" title="Marianne Moore">Moore</a>), she does not give us an image, however fractured, of a carafe on a table; rather, she forces us to reconsider how language actually constructs the world we know."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perloff_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Perloff-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alice_B._Toklas">Alice B. Toklas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Alice B. Toklas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Stein met her <a href="/wiki/Life_partner" title="Life partner">life partner</a> <a href="/wiki/Alice_B._Toklas" title="Alice B. Toklas">Alice B. Toklas</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alice_Toklas_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alice_Toklas-2">[2]</a></sup> on September 8, 1907, on Toklas's first day in Paris, at Sarah and Michael Stein's apartment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> On meeting Stein, Toklas wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>She was a golden brown presence, burned by the Tuscan sun and with a golden glint in her warm brown hair. She was dressed in a warm brown corduroy suit. She wore a large round coral brooch and when she talked, very little, or laughed, a good deal, I thought her voice came from this brooch. It was unlike anyone else's voiceâdeep, full, velvety, like a great contralto's, like two voices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Soon thereafter, Stein introduced Toklas to <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a> at his studio, where he was at work on <i><a href="/wiki/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon" title="Les Demoiselles d'Avignon">Les Demoiselles d'Avignon</a></i>.</p>
<p>In 1908, they summered in <a href="/wiki/Fiesole" title="Fiesole">Fiesole</a>, Italy, Toklas staying with <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Lane_Levy" title="Harriet Lane Levy">Harriet Lane Levy</a>, the companion of her trip from the United States, and her housemate until Alice moved in with Stein and Leo in 1910. That summer, Stein stayed with Michael and Sarah Stein, their son Allan, and Leo in a nearby villa. Gertrude and Alice's summer of 1908 is memorialized in images of the two of them in Venice, at the piazza in front of Saint Mark's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mellow_1974.2C_p._122_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mellow_1974.2C_p._122-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Toklas arrived in 1907 with Harriet Levy, with Toklas maintaining living arrangements with Levy until she moved to 27 Rue de Fleurus in 1910. In an essay written at the time, Stein humorously discussed the complex efforts, involving much letter writing and Victorian niceties, to extricate Levy from Toklas's living arrangements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> In "Harriet", Stein considers Levy's nonexistent plans for the summer, following her nonexistent plans for the winter:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>She said she did not have any plans for the summer. No one was interested in this thing in whether she had any plans for the summer. That is not the complete history of this thing, some were interested in this thing in her not having any plans for the summer... Some who were not interested in her not having made plans for the summer were interested in her not having made plans for the following winter. She had not made plans for the summer and she had not made plans for the following winter... There was then coming to be the end of the summer and she was then not answering anything when any one asked her what were her plans for the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStein1934105.E2.80.9307_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStein1934105.E2.80.9307-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gertrude_Stein_by_Alvin_Langdon_Coburn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="925" data-file-width="715" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Gertrude_Stein_by_Alvin_Langdon_Coburn.jpg/170px-Gertrude_Stein_by_Alvin_Langdon_Coburn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Gertrude_Stein_by_Alvin_Langdon_Coburn.jpg/255px-Gertrude_Stein_by_Alvin_Langdon_Coburn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Gertrude_Stein_by_Alvin_Langdon_Coburn.jpg/340px-Gertrude_Stein_by_Alvin_Langdon_Coburn.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gertrude_Stein_by_Alvin_Langdon_Coburn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stein in 1913</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the early summer of 1914, Gertrude bought three paintings by <a href="/wiki/Juan_Gris" title="Juan Gris">Juan Gris</a>: <i>Roses</i>, <i>Glass and Bottle</i>, and <i>Book and Glasses</i>. Soon after she purchased them from <a href="/wiki/Daniel-Henry_Kahnweiler" title="Daniel-Henry Kahnweiler">Daniel-Henry Kahnweiler</a>'s gallery,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> the Great War began, Kahnweiler's stock was confiscated and he was not allowed to return to Paris. Gris, who before the war had entered a binding contract with Kahnweiler for his output, was left without income. Gertrude attempted to enter an ancillary arrangement in which she would forward Gris living expenses in exchange for future pictures. Stein and Toklas had plans to visit England to sign a contract for the publication of <i>Three Lives</i>, to spend a few weeks there, and then journey to Spain. They left Paris on July 6, 1914 and returned on October 17.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> When Britain declared war on Germany, Stein and Toklas were visiting <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred North Whitehead</a> in England. After a supposed three-week trip to England that stretched to three months due to the War, they returned to France, where they spent the first winter of the war.</p>
<p>With money acquired from the sale of Stein's last Matisse <i><a href="/wiki/Woman_with_a_Hat" title="Woman with a Hat">Woman with a Hat</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> to her brother Michael, she and Toklas vacationed in Spain from May 1915 through the spring of 1916.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> During their interlude in <a href="/wiki/Majorca" title="Majorca">Majorca</a>, Spain, Gertrude continued her correspondence with <a href="/wiki/Mildred_Aldrich" title="Mildred Aldrich">Mildred Aldrich</a> who kept her apprised of the War's progression, and eventually inspired Gertrude and Alice to return to France to join the war effort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
