<p>Toklas and Stein returned to Paris in June 1916, and acquired a Ford automobile with the help of associates in the United States; Gertrude learned to drive it with the help of her friend <a href="/wiki/William_Edwards_Cook" title="William Edwards Cook">William Edwards Cook</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Gertrude and Alice then volunteered to drive supplies to French hospitals, in the Ford they named <i>Auntie</i>, "after Gertrude's aunt Pauline, 'who always behaved admirably in emergencies and behaved fairly well most times if she was flattered.'"</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GertrudeStein_JackHemingway_Paris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="676" data-file-width="647" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/GertrudeStein_JackHemingway_Paris.jpg/220px-GertrudeStein_JackHemingway_Paris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/GertrudeStein_JackHemingway_Paris.jpg/330px-GertrudeStein_JackHemingway_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/GertrudeStein_JackHemingway_Paris.jpg/440px-GertrudeStein_JackHemingway_Paris.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GertrudeStein_JackHemingway_Paris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gertrude Stein with <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a>'s son, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Hemingway" title="Jack Hemingway">Jack Hemingway</a> in 1924. Stein is credited with bringing the term "Lost Generation" into use.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the 1930s, Stein and Toklas became famous with the 1933 mass market publication of <i>The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas.</i> She and Alice had an extended lecture tour in the United States during this decade. They also spent several summers in the town of Bilignin, in the <a href="/wiki/Ain" title="Ain">Ain</a> district of eastern France situated in the picturesque region of the <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne-Alpes" title="RhÃ´ne-Alpes">RhÃ´ne-Alpes</a>. The two women doted on their beloved poodle named "Basket" whose successor, "Basket II", comforted Alice in the years after Gertrude's death.</p>
<p>With the outbreak of World War II, Stein and Toklas relocated to a country home that they had rented for many years previously in Bilignin, <a href="/wiki/Ain" title="Ain">Ain</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne-Alpes" title="RhÃ´ne-Alpes">RhÃ´ne-Alpes</a> region. Gertrude and Alice, who were both Jewish, escaped persecution probably because of their friendship to <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Fa%C3%BF" title="Bernard FaÃ¿">Bernard FaÃ¿</a> who was a collaborator with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vichy_regime" title="Vichy regime">Vichy regime</a> and had connections to the <a href="/wiki/Gestapo" title="Gestapo">Gestapo</a>, or possibly because Gertrude was an American and a famous author. Gertrude's book "Wars I Have Seen" written before the German surrender and before the liberation of German concentration camps, likened the German army to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keystone_cops" title="Keystone cops">Keystone cops</a>. When FaÃ¿ was sentenced to hard labor for life after the war, Gertrude and Alice campaigned for his release. Several years later, Toklas would contribute money to FaÃ¿'s escape from prison. After the war, Stein was visited by many young American soldiers. The August 6, 1945 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i> magazine featured a photo of Stein and American soldiers posing in front of Hitler's bunker in <a href="/wiki/Berchtesgaden" title="Berchtesgaden">Berchtesgaden</a>. They are all giving the Nazi salute and Stein is wearing the traditional Alpine cap, accompanied by the text: "Off We All Went To See Germany."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1980s, a cabinet in the <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beinecke_Library" title="Beinecke Library">Beinecke Library</a>, which had been locked for an indeterminate number of years, was opened and found to contain some 300 love letters written by Stein and Toklas. They were made public for the first time, revealing intimate details of their relationship. Stein's endearment for Toklas was "Baby Precious", in turn Stein was for Toklas, "Mr. Cuddle-Wuddle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lesbian_relationships">Lesbian relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Lesbian relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Stein is the author of one of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Coming_out" title="Coming out">coming out</a> stories, "<i>Q.E.D</i>" (published in 1950 as <i>Things as They Are</i>), written in 1903 and suppressed by the author. The story, written during travels after leaving college, is based on a three-person romantic affair in which she became involved while studying at <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins</a> in Baltimore. The affair was complicated, as Stein was less experienced with the social dynamics of <a href="/wiki/Romantic_friendship" title="Romantic friendship">romantic friendship</a> as well as her own sexuality and any moral dilemmas regarding it. Stein maintained at the time that she detested "passion in its many disguised forms". The relationships of Stein's acquaintances Mabel Haynes and Grace Lounsbury ended as Haynes started one with <a href="/wiki/Mary_Bookstaver" title="Mary Bookstaver">Mary Bookstaver</a> (also known as May Bookstaver). Stein became enamored of Bookstaver but was unsuccessful in advancing their relationship. Bookstaver, Haynes, and Lounsbury all later married men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackmer_1995_pp._681_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blackmer_1995_pp._681-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stein began to accept and define her pseudo-masculinity through the ideas of <a href="/wiki/Otto_Weininger" title="Otto Weininger">Otto Weininger</a>'s <i>Sex and Character</i> (1906). Weininger, though <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> by birth, considered Jewish men effeminate and women as incapable of selfhood and genius, except for female homosexuals who may approximate masculinity. As Stein equated genius with masculinity, her position as a female and an intellectual becomes difficult to synthesize and modern feminist interpretations of her work have been called into question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramsay_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramsay-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>More positive affirmations of Stein's sexuality began with her relationship with <a href="/wiki/Alice_B._Toklas" title="Alice B. Toklas">Alice B. Toklas</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a> describes how Alice was Gertrude's "wife" in that Stein rarely addressed his (Hemingway's) wife, and he treated Alice the same, leaving the two "wives" to chat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grahn_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grahn-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>The more affirming essay "Miss Furr and Miss Skeene" is one of the first homosexual revelation stories to be published. The work, like <i>Q.E.D.</i>, is informed by Stein's growing involvement with a homosexual community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grahn_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grahn-93">[93]</a></sup> though it is based on lesbian partners Maud Hunt Squire and Ethel Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackmer_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blackmer-5">[5]</a></sup> The work contains the word "<a href="/wiki/Gay" title="Gay">gay</a>" over one hundred times, perhaps the first published use of the word "gay" in reference to same-sex relationships and those who have them,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackmer_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Blackmer-5">[5]</a></sup> and, thus, uninformed readers missed the lesbian content. A similar essay of homosexual men begins more obviously with the line "Sometimes men are kissing" but is less well known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackmer_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Blackmer-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tender_buttons:_objects,_food,_rooms" title="Tender buttons: objects, food, rooms">Tender Buttons</a></i> Stein comments on lesbian sexuality and the work abounds with "highly condensed layers of public and private meanings" created by wordplay including puns on the words "box", "cow", and in titles such as "tender buttons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackmer_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Blackmer-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id=".22There_is_no_there_there.22">"There is no there there"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title='Edit section: "There is no there there"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Along with Stein's widely known "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_rose_is_a_rose_is_a_rose_is_a_rose" title="A rose is a rose is a rose is a rose">A rose is a rose is a rose is a rose</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> quotation, "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/There_is_no_there_there" title="There is no there there">there is no there there</a>" is also one of her most famous. It appears in <i>Gertrude Stein, Everybody's Autobiography</i> (Random House 1937, p 289) and is often applied to the city of her childhood, Oakland, California. Defenders and critics of Oakland have debated what she really meant when she said this in 1933, after coming to San Francisco on a book tour. She took a ferry to Oakland to visit the farm she grew up on, and the house she lived in near what is now 13th Avenue and E. 25th Street in Oakland. The house had been razed, and the farmland had been developed with new housing in the three decades since her father had sold the property and moved closer to the commercial hub of the neighborhood on Washington Street (now 12th Avenue). She wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>She took us to see her granddaughter who was teaching in the Dominican convent in San Raphael, we went across the bay on a ferry, that had not changed but Goat Island might just as well not have been there, anyway what was the use of my having come from Oakland it was not natural to have come from there yes write about it if I like or anything if I like but not there, there is no there there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>...but not there, there is no there there. ... Ah Thirteenth Avenue was the same it was shabby and overgrown. ... Not of course the house, the house the big house and the big garden and the eucalyptus trees and the rose hedge naturally were not there any longer existing, what was the use ...</p>
<p>It is a funny thing about addresses where you live. When you live there you know it so well that it is like an identity a thing that is so much a thing that it could not ever be any other thing and then you live somewhere else and years later, the address that was so much an address that it was like your name and you said it as if it was not an address but something that was living and then years after you do not know what the address was and when you say it is not a name anymore but something you cannot remember. That is what makes your identity not a thing that exists but something you do or do not remember.</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_views">Political views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Political views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to Janet Malcolm's contested account in <i>Two Lives: Gertrude and Alice</i>, Stein was a vocal critic of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt">Franklin Delano Roosevelt</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-entertainment.timesonline.co.uk_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-entertainment.timesonline.co.uk-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>While some have stressed her queer, feminist, pro-immigration, and democratic politics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C4WDefault-4711487_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-C4WDefault-4711487-100">[100]</a></sup> her statements on immigration include sentiments that would be considered racist today. In a 1934 interview published in <i>The New York Times</i> she stated:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>That is the reason why I do not approve of the stringent immigration laws in America today. We need the stimulation of new blood. It is best to favor healthy competition. There is no reason why we should not select our immigrants with greater care, nor why we should not bar certain peoples and preserve the color line for instance. But if we shut down on immigration completely we shall become stagnant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>She publicly endorsed General <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> and admired Vichy leader Marshal <a href="/wiki/Philippe_P%C3%A9tain" title="Philippe PÃ©tain">Philippe PÃ©tain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-entertainment.timesonline.co.uk_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-entertainment.timesonline.co.uk-96">[96]</a></sup> Some have argued for a more nuanced view of Stein's collaborationist activity, arguing that it was rooted in her wartime predicament and status as a Jew in Nazi-occupied France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Similarly, Stein commented at 1938 on <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leon_Trotsky" title="Leon Trotsky">Leon Trotsky</a>: "There is too much fathering going on just now and there is no doubt about it fathers are depressing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackmer_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-Blackmer-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_activities">World War II activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: World War II activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While identified with the modernist movements in art and literature, Stein's political affiliations were a mix of reactionary and progressive ideas. She was outspoken in her hostility to some liberal reforms of progressive politics. To Stein, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> had acted as a negative societal force, disrupting stability, degrading values, and subsequently affecting cultural decline. Stein idealized the 18th century as the golden age of civilization, epitomized in America as the era of its founding fathers and what was in France, the glory of its pre-revolutionary <a href="/wiki/Ancien_R%C3%A9gime" title="Ancien RÃ©gime">Ancien RÃ©gime</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> At the same time, she was pro-immigrant, pro-democratic, and anti-patriarchal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Her last major work was the <a href="/wiki/Libretto" title="Libretto">libretto</a> of the feminist opera <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mother_of_Us_All" title="The Mother of Us All">The Mother of Us All</a></i> (1947) about the socially progressive suffragette movement and another work from this time, <i>Brewsie and Willie</i> (1946), expressed strong support for American G.I.s.</p>
<p>A compendium of source material confirms that Stein may have been able to save her life and sustain her lifestyle through the protection of powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vichy_government" title="Vichy government">Vichy government</a> official <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Fa%C3%BF" title="Bernard FaÃ¿">Bernard FaÃ¿</a>. Stein had met FaÃ¿ in 1926, and he became her "dearest friend during her life", according to Alice B. Toklas. FaÃ¿ had been the primary translator of Stein's work into French and subsequently masterminded her 1933â34 American book tour, which gave Stein celebrity status and proved to be a highly successful promotion of her memoir, <i>The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[6]</a></sup> FaÃ¿'s influence was instrumental in avoiding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi" title="Nazi">Nazi</a> confiscation of Stein's historically significant and monetarily valuable collection of artwork, which throughout the war years was housed in Stein's Paris rue Christine apartment, under locked safeguard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newyorker.com_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-newyorker.com-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1941, at FaÃ¿'s suggestion, Stein consented to translate into English some 180 pages of speeches made by Marshal <a href="/wiki/Philippe_P%C3%A9tain" title="Philippe PÃ©tain">Philippe PÃ©tain</a>. In her introduction, Stein crafts an analogy between <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> and PÃ©tain. She writes of the high esteem in which PÃ©tain is held by his countrymen; France respected and admired the man who had struck an armistice with Hitler. Conceived and targeted for an American readership, Stein's translations were ultimately never published in the United States. <a href="/wiki/Random_House" title="Random House">Random House</a> publisher <a href="/wiki/Bennett_Cerf" title="Bennett Cerf">Bennett Cerf</a> had read the introduction Stein had written for the translations and been horrified by what she had produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nybooks.com_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-nybooks.com-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Jewish, Stein collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy France</a>, a regime that deported more than 75,000 Jews to <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">Nazi concentration camps</a>, of whom only 3 percent survived the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> In 1944, Stein wrote that Petain's policies were "really wonderful so simple so natural so extraordinary". This was Stein's contention in the year when the town of <a href="/wiki/Culoz" title="Culoz">Culoz</a>, where she and Toklas resided, saw the removal of its Jewish children to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auschwitz" title="Auschwitz">Auschwitz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-90">[90]</a></sup> It is difficult to say, however, how aware Stein was of these events. As she wrote in <i>Wars I Have Seen</i>, "However near a war is it is always not very near. Even when it is here."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Stein had stopped translating Petain's speeches three years previously, in 1941.</p>
<p>Stein was able to condemn the Japanese attack at <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a> while simultaneously maintaining the dissonant acceptance of Hitler as conqueror of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[6]</a></sup> Journalist Lanning Warren interviewed Stein in her Paris apartment in a piece published in <i>The New York Times Magazine</i> on May 6, 1934. Stein, seemingly ironically, proclaimed that Hitler merited the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a>.</p>
<dl>
<dd>"The Saxon element is always destined to be dominated. The Germans have no gift at organizing. They can only obey. And obedience is not organization. Organization comes from community of will as well as community of action. And in America our democracy has been based on community of will and effort.... I say Hitler ought to have the peace prize...because he is removing all elements of contest and struggle from Germany. By driving out the Jews and the democratic Left elements, he is driving out everything that conduces to activity. That means peace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newyorker.com_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-newyorker.com-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nybooks.com_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-nybooks.com-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Given that after the war Stein commented that the only way to ensure world peace was to teach the Germans disobedience,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> this 1934 Stein interview has come to be interpreted as an ironic jest made by a practiced iconoclast hoping to gain attention and provoke controversy. In an effort to correct popular mainstream misrepresentations of Stein's wartime activity, a dossier of articles by critics and historians has been gathered for the online journal Jacket2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacket2.org_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacket2.org-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>How much of Stein's wartime activities were motivated by the real exigencies of self-preservation in a dangerous environment can only be speculated upon. However, her loyalty to PÃ©tain may have gone beyond expedience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nybooks.com_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-nybooks.com-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacket2.org_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-jacket2.org-114">[114]</a></sup> She had been urged to leave France by American embassy officials, friends and family when that possibility still existed, but declined to do so. Accustomed to a life of entitlement since birth, Stein may have been convinced her wealth and notoriety would exempt her from what had befallen other European Jews. In an essay written for the <i>Atlantic Monthly</i> in November, 1940, Stein had written about her decision not to leave France: "it would be awfully uncomfortable and I am fussy about my food." Stein continued to praise PÃ©tain after the war ended, this at a time when PÃ©tain had been sentenced to death by a French court for treason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Author <a href="/wiki/Djuna_Barnes" title="Djuna Barnes">Djuna Barnes</a> provided a caustic assessment of Stein's book, "Wars I Have Seen":</p>
<dl>
<dd>"You do not feel that she [Stein] is ever really worried about the sorrows of the people. Her concerns at its highest pitch is a well-fed apprehension."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-90">[90]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Others have argued that some of the accounts of Stein's war time activities have amounted to a "witch hunt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C4WDefault-7643746_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-C4WDefault-7643746-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Stein died on July 27, 1946 at the age of 72 after surgery for <a href="/wiki/Stomach_cancer" title="Stomach cancer">stomach cancer</a> at the local hospital in <a href="/wiki/Neuilly-sur-Seine" title="Neuilly-sur-Seine">Neuilly-sur-Seine</a>. She was interred in Paris in <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A8re_Lachaise_Cemetery" title="PÃ¨re Lachaise Cemetery">PÃ¨re Lachaise Cemetery</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> According to the famous version of her last moments, before having been taken into surgery, Stein asked her partner Toklas: "What is the answer?" After Toklas replied to Stein that there was no answer, Stein countered by sinking back into her bed, murmuring: "Then, there is no question!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her companion Toklas, however, has given two other versions of the encounterâneither of which agrees with the "canonical" version above. Writing in the June 2005 edition of <i>The New Yorker</i>, Janet Malcolm describes:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>On July 27, 1946, Stein was operated on for what proved to be inoperable stomach cancer and died before coming out of anesthesia. In "What Is Remembered," Toklas wrote of the "troubled, confused and very uncertain" afternoon of the surgery. "I sat next to her and she said to me early in the afternoon, What is the answer? I was silent. In that case, she said, what is the question?" However, in a letter to Van Vechten ten years earlier, Toklas had written:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>About Baby's last words. She said upon waking from a sleep--What is the question. And I didn't answer thinking she was not completely awakened. Then she said again--What is the question and before I could speak she went on--If there is no question then there is no answer.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Stein's biographers have naturally selected the superior "in that case what is the question?" version. Strong narratives win out over weak ones when no obstacle of factuality stands in their way. What Stein actually said remains unknown. That Toklas cited the lesser version in a letter of 1953 is suggestive but not conclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Stein named writer and photographer <a href="/wiki/Carl_Van_Vechten" title="Carl Van Vechten">Carl Van Vechten</a> as her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_executor" title="Literary executor">literary executor</a>, and he helped to publish works of hers that remained unpublished at the time of her death. <a class="external text" href="http://www.bryantpark.org/things-to-do/monuments.html" rel="nofollow">There is a monument to Stein</a> on the Upper Terrace of <a href="/wiki/Bryant_Park" title="Bryant Park">Bryant Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Critical reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sherwood_Anderson" title="Sherwood Anderson">Sherwood Anderson</a> in his public introduction to Stein's 1922 publication of <i>Geography and Plays</i> wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>For me the work of Gertrude Stein consists in a rebuilding, an entirely new recasting of life, in the city of words. Here is one artist who has been able to accept ridicule, who has even forgone the privilege of writing the great American novel, uplifting our English speaking stage, and wearing the bays of the great poets to go live among the little housekeeping words, the swaggering bullying street-corner words, the honest working, money saving words and all the other forgotten and neglected citizens of the sacred and half forgotten city.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In a private letter to his brother Karl, Anderson said, "As for Stein, I do not think her too important. I do think she had an important thing to do, not for the public, but for the artist who happens to work with words as his material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMellow1974260_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMellow1974260-117">[117]</a></sup>"</p>
<p>Other critics took a more negative view of Stein's work. F. W. Dupee (1990, p.Â IX) defines "Steinese" as "gnomic, repetitive, illogical, sparsely punctuated... a scandal and a delight, lending itself equally to derisory parody and fierce denunciation".</p>
<p>Composer Constant Lambert (1936) compares Stravinsky's choice of "the drabbest and least significant phrases" in <i>L'Histoire du Soldat</i> to Gertrude Stein's in "Helen Furr and Georgine Skeene" (1922), specifically: "[E]veryday they were gay there, they were regularly gay there everyday." He writes that the "effect would be equally appreciated by someone with no knowledge of English whatsoever", apparently missing the pun frequently employed by Stein.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/James_Thurber" title="James Thurber">James Thurber</a> wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Anyone who reads at all diversely during these bizarre 1920s cannot escape the conclusion that a number of crazy men and women are writing stuff which remarkably passes for important composition among certain persons who should know better. Stuart P. Sherman, however, refused to be numbered among those who stand in awe and admiration of one of the most eminent of the idiots, Gertrude Stein. He reviews her <i>Geography and Plays</i> in the August 11 issue of the <i>Literary Review</i> of the <i>New York Evening Post</i> and arrives at the conviction that it is a marvellous and painstaking achievement in setting down approximately 80,000 words which mean nothing at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Author <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Katherine_Ann_Porter" title="Katherine Ann Porter">Katherine Ann Porter</a> provided her own estimation of Stein's literary legacy: "Wise or silly or nothing at all, down everything goes on the page with an air of everything being equal, unimportant in itself important because it happened to her and she was writing about it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benstock.2C_Shari_1987_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benstock.2C_Shari_1987-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>History Professor <a href="/wiki/Blanche_Wiesen_Cook" title="Blanche Wiesen Cook">Blanche Wiesen Cook</a>, has written of Stein: "She was not a radical feminist. She was Jewish and anti-Semitic, lesbian and contemptuous of women, ignorant about economics and hostile to socialism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benstock.2C_Shari_1987_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Benstock.2C_Shari_1987-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Writing for <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vanity_Fair_(American_magazine_1913%E2%80%931936)" title="Vanity Fair (American magazine 1913â1936)">Vanity Fair</a></i> magazine in 1923, eminent literary critic <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Wilson" title="Edmund Wilson">Edmund Wilson</a> presciently came to an evaluation similar to the one made by Katharine Ann Porter some twenty years later, after Stein's death. Wilson deemed that Stein's technique was one of flawed methodology, using words analogous to the way <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cubists" title="Cubists">Cubists</a> manipulated abstract forms in their artworks. As Wilson wrote, unlike the <a href="/wiki/Plastic_arts" title="Plastic arts">plastic arts</a>, literature deals with</p>
<dl>
<dd>"human speech [which] is a tissue of ideas....Miss Stein no longer understands the conditions under which literary effects have to be produced...There is sometimes genuine music in the most baffling of her works, but there are rarely any communicated emotions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson.2C_Edmund_1923.2C_p._878-879_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson.2C_Edmund_1923.2C_p._878-879-120">[120]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>An elevated observer, perched high above everything below, he likened Stein to a self-conceived "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a>...registering impressions like some august seismograph".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson.2C_Edmund_1923.2C_p._878-879_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson.2C_Edmund_1923.2C_p._878-879-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stein's literary output was a subject of amusement for her brother Leo Stein, who characterized her writing as an "abomination". Later detractors of Stein's work deemed her experimentation as the serendipitous result of her alleged inability to communicate through linguistic convention, deficient in the skills required "to deal effectively with language, so that she made her greatest weakness into her most remarkable strength".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benstock.2C_Shari_1987_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-Benstock.2C_Shari_1987-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_commemoration">Legacy and commemoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gertrude_Stein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Legacy and commemoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Stein has been the subject of many artistic works. Stein and Toklas merit their own line each in the song "Bosom Buddies" from the 1966 Broadway musical <i>MAME</i>, based on the stage play <i>Auntie Mame</i>, by composer-lyricist Jerry Herman. In <i>MAME</i>, Vera Charles, Mame Dennis' actress-confidante pal, sings: "...I'll always be Alice Toklas, if you'll be Gertrude Stein." (Bea Arthur, who played the original Vera Charles on Broadway, recreated the same role for the 1974 film version of the musical.) In the 1998 Latin American literary classic <i>Yo-Yo Boing!</i>, novelist <a href="/wiki/Giannina_Braschi" title="Giannina Braschi">Giannina Braschi</a> pays homage to Stein as an imaginary mentor.</p>
<p>In 2005, playwright/actor <a href="/wiki/Jade_Esteban_Estrada" title="Jade Esteban Estrada">Jade Esteban Estrada</a> portrayed Stein in the solo musical <i>ICONS: The Lesbian and Gay History of the World, Vol. 1</i> at <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>. In 2006, theatre director/actor <a href="/wiki/Luiz_P%C3%A4etow" title="Luiz PÃ¤etow">Luiz PÃ¤etow</a> created his solo, <i>Plays</i>, portraying Stein's 1934 homonymous lecture, and toured <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> for several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> <i>Loving Repeating</i> is a musical by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Flaherty" title="Stephen Flaherty">Stephen Flaherty</a> based on the writings of Gertrude Stein. Stein and <a href="/wiki/Alice_B._Toklas" title="Alice B. Toklas">Alice B. Toklas</a> are both characters in the eight person show. Stein is a central character in <a href="/wiki/Nick_Bertozzi" title="Nick Bertozzi">Nick Bertozzi</a>'s 2007 graphic novel <i>The Salon</i>.</p>
<p>The posthumously published <i><a href="/wiki/Journals_of_Ayn_Rand" title="Journals of Ayn Rand">Journals of Ayn Rand</a></i> contain several highly hostile references to Gertrude Stein. From Rand's working notes for her novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fountainhead" title="The Fountainhead">The Fountainhead</a></i>, it is clear that the character Lois Cook in that book was intended as a caricature of Stein.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Stein (played by <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Cribbins" title="Bernard Cribbins">Bernard Cribbins</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Alice_B._Toklas" title="Alice B. Toklas">Toklas</a> (played by <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid_Brambell" title="Wilfrid Brambell">Wilfrid Brambell</a>) were depicted in the playful <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Swedish</a> 1978 <a href="/wiki/Absurdist_fiction" title="Absurdist fiction">absurdist fiction</a> film <i>Picassos Ã¤ventyr</i> <i>(<a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Picasso" title="The Adventures of Picasso">The Adventures of Picasso</a>)</i> by director <a href="/wiki/Tage_Danielsson" title="Tage Danielsson">Tage Danielsson</a>, with <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6sta_Ekman" title="GÃ¶sta Ekman">GÃ¶sta Ekman</a> as <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Picasso</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stein was also portrayed in the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Woody_Allen" title="Woody Allen">Woody Allen</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Midnight_in_Paris" title="Midnight in Paris">Midnight in Paris</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Kathy_Bates" title="Kathy Bates">Kathy Bates</a>. Her name is added to a list of great artists and notables in the popular Broadway musical <i><a href="/wiki/Rent_(musical)" title="Rent (musical)">Rent</a></i> in the song "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Vie_Boheme" title="La Vie Boheme">La Vie Boheme</a>". She is also mentioned in the Fred Astaire - Ginger Rogers 1935 film <i>Top Hat</i> and in the song "<a href="/wiki/Roseability" title="Roseability">Roseability</a>" by the Scottish rock group <a href="/wiki/Idlewild_(band)" title="Idlewild (band)">Idlewild</a>.</p>
<p>Composer <a href="/wiki/Ricky_Ian_Gordon" title="Ricky Ian Gordon">Ricky Ian Gordon</a> and librettist <a href="/wiki/Royce_Vavrek" title="Royce Vavrek">Royce Vavrek</a>'s opera <i><a href="/wiki/27_(opera)" title="27 (opera)">27</a></i> about Stein and Toklas premiered at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opera_Theatre_of_St._Louis" title="Opera Theatre of St. Louis">Opera Theatre of St. Louis</a> in June, 2014 with <a href="/wiki/Stephanie_Blythe" title="Stephanie Blythe">Stephanie Blythe</a> as Stein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first, the Paris Autumn Salon of 1905, introduced <a href="/wiki/Fauvism" title="Fauvism">Fauvism</a> to the Paris art public, to some shock and political cartooning.
